        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548"><meta property="og:title" content="Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO2 Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/acsomega.7b00548.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="TiO2 anatase has emerged as a promising anode for Na-ion batteries (SIBs). However, widespread use of this anode is severely limited by a series of factors that need to be identified and understood to further improve their electrochemical response. Here, we have taken benefit from the versatility of a self-assembly seeding-assisted method to obtain a variety of uniform high-surface-area undoped TiO2 anatase nanostructures. Electrodes built from these uniform nanostructures in combination with a safe ionic liquid electrolyte have allowed a systematic study on some of the factors that determine the electrochemical activity of Na-ion anatase anodes. Interestingly, the inherent low penetrability of the ionic liquid electrolyte has resulted in an unexpected asset to clarify large differences in Na+ uptake by different nanostructures. Basically, solid electrolyte interface (SEI) effects were maximized and therefore clearly separated from electrochemical reactions strictly associated with the anatase anode. Thus, for electrodes built from nanostructures that preserved their initial conformation after cycling, the first discharge showed Na+ uptakes well-beyond those of the Ti4+/Ti3+ redox couple. This large uptake has been associated with an apparent reversible reaction that operates below ca. 0.5–0.7 V and an irreversible mechanism that operates at lower voltages (ca. 0.3 V). However, for electrodes built from nanostructures that favored SEI formation, the irreversible reaction associated with the plateau at ca. 0.3 V was not observed during the first discharge. In accordance, the total Na+ uptake did not reach values beyond those of the corresponding Ti4+/Ti3+ redox couple. Irreversibility, in this case, is associated with SEI formation. Our results also establish the strong effect that size at different scale levels has in the electrochemical response of anatase anodes for SIBs (changes from ca. 6 to 11 nm in crystal sizes and from 50 to 80 in nanostructure sizes led to pronounced differences). This result emphasizes that any conclusions on mechanistic studies other than size effects must be done under strict control on size at various scales (size as a strict control variable at crystal level and nanostructure or in more general terms aggregate scale levels). Finally, we have found that at 30 and 60 °C the performance of the best of the electrodes, with the low-flammable and low-volatile ionic liquid electrolyte, is comparable to that of similar nanostructures immersed in their Li-ion electrolyte counterparts. This result is promising, as in stationary applications where SIBs could replace Li-ion batteries, large accumulation of storage components imposes more strict safety criteria. Basically, power criteria can be relaxed in response to more strict safety criteria."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/acsomega.7b00548.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '346b8fc9-08b6-45f3-9924-575cbf93c01b'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'omega';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/omega/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/omega/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00548&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "acsodf";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Toward a Better Understanding and Optimization of the Electrochemical Activity of Na-Ion TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Anatase Anodes Using Uniform Nanostructures and Ionic Liquid Electrolytes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00548", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0002.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">TiO<sub>2</sub> anatase has emerged as a promising anode for Na-ion batteries (SIBs). However, widespread use of this anode is severely limited by a series of factors that need to be identified and understood to further improve their electrochemical response. Here, we have taken benefit from the versatility of a self-assembly seeding-assisted method to obtain a variety of uniform high-surface-area undoped TiO<sub>2</sub> anatase nanostructures. Electrodes built from these uniform nanostructures in combination with a safe ionic liquid electrolyte have allowed a systematic study on some of the factors that determine the electrochemical activity of Na-ion anatase anodes. Interestingly, the inherent low penetrability of the ionic liquid electrolyte has resulted in an unexpected asset to clarify large differences in Na<sup>+</sup> uptake by different nanostructures. Basically, solid electrolyte interface (SEI) effects were maximized and therefore clearly separated from electrochemical reactions strictly associated with the anatase anode. Thus, for electrodes built from nanostructures that preserved their initial conformation after cycling, the first discharge showed Na<sup>+</sup> uptakes well-beyond those of the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple. This large uptake has been associated with an apparent reversible reaction that operates below ca. 0.5–0.7 V and an irreversible mechanism that operates at lower voltages (ca. 0.3 V). However, for electrodes built from nanostructures that favored SEI formation, the irreversible reaction associated with the plateau at ca. 0.3 V was not observed during the first discharge. In accordance, the total Na<sup>+</sup> uptake did not reach values beyond those of the corresponding Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple. Irreversibility, in this case, is associated with SEI formation. Our results also establish the strong effect that size at different scale levels has in the electrochemical response of anatase anodes for SIBs (changes from ca. 6 to 11 nm in crystal sizes and from 50 to 80 in nanostructure sizes led to pronounced differences). This result emphasizes that any conclusions on mechanistic studies other than size effects must be done under strict control on size at various scales (size as a strict control variable at crystal level and nanostructure or in more general terms aggregate scale levels). Finally, we have found that at 30 and 60 °C the performance of the best of the electrodes, with the low-flammable and low-volatile ionic liquid electrolyte, is comparable to that of similar nanostructures immersed in their Li-ion electrolyte counterparts. This result is promising, as in stationary applications where SIBs could replace Li-ion batteries, large accumulation of storage components imposes more strict safety criteria. Basically, power criteria can be relaxed in response to more strict safety criteria.</p></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes"> Electrolytes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291999&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Minerals"> Minerals </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292416&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanostructures"> Nanostructures </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292274&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Surface chemistry"> Surface chemistry </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are being explored as a possible alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Sodium reserves are abundant and importantly widely spread, whereas lithium is scarce and localized in remote or conflictive areas. Furthermore, unlike lithium, sodium does not form an alloy with aluminum, enabling the use of this metal as a current collector. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> However, Na-ions are heavier than Li-ions and significantly larger in size (102 vs 76 pm). Thus, one could expect that electrodes active to both Li- and Na-ions show on average a poorer performance for Na-ions. These straightforward arguments suggest that SIBs could replace LIBs in stationary applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">To a certain extent, there has been relative success in obtaining cathodes for SIBs with good stability using inexpensive raw materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Furthermore, there are now examples of cathodes showing excellent rate capabilities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Thus, and regarding electrode materials, the development of SIBs greatly depends on the development of adequate negative electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Studies on different TiO<sub>2</sub> polymorphs have demonstrated their capabilities as a potential negative electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 5 to 43">(5-43)</a> More specifically, substantial and significant reports have shown the relatively good capabilities of the TiO<sub>2</sub> anatase polymorph as anodes for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 6, 16 to 18 and 20 to 43">(6, 16-18, 20-43)</a> These anodes, working at a relatively low average potential versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, could assure at least acceptable energy density using an abundant material. A summary of some of the characteristics of anatase anodes reported earlier is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> (see the Supporting Information). For example, a common characteristic of these anodes is the irreversibility observed after the first discharge curve, specially manifested at slow rates where all possible electrochemical processes are active. The source of this irreversibility and, in general, the mechanism of sodiation/desodiation by anatase anodes are still a matter of continuous research. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16-18)</a> Recent studies indicate that in anatase, insertion of 1 Na<sup>+</sup> is feasible, leading to the formation of a highly disordered phase (layered-like rhombohedral <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> structure). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Upon desodiation, this structure seems to reverse to a structure similar to anatase but with additional amorphization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Interestingly, in this study, the total uptake during the first discharge was about 1.1 Na<sup>+</sup>, relatively close to that from the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple. However, values well-beyond 1 Na<sup>+</sup> have been reported during the first discharge, especially when working with nanosize doped anatases (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref16 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref25 ref28 ref29 ref31 ref32 ref35 ref39 ref40 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref16 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref25 ref28 ref29 ref31 ref32 ref35 ref39 ref40 ref42" aria-label="reference 6, 16, 20 to 23, 25, 28, 29, 31, 32, 35, 39, 40 and 42">(6, 16, 20-23, 25, 28, 29, 31, 32, 35, 39, 40, 42)</a> These high values (even uptakes of 3.5 Na<sup>+</sup> are reported) suggest that in addition to an insertion mechanism other mechanisms could also be operative, such as the conversion reaction proposed by Passerini and co-workers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 and 18">(16, 18)</a> However, the activity of these anodes takes place below 1 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, where solid electrolyte interfaces (SEIs) are formed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 16, 17 and 44 to 46">(16, 17, 44-46)</a> Obviously, as Na<sup>+</sup> uptake values well-beyond those of the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple are linked to the electrodes built from nanosized anatases, there is always uncertainty on the contribution of SEIs during the first cycles (especially the first discharge). Furthermore, SEIs in SIBs show in general lower stability than that of their Li-ion counterparts. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref47" aria-label="reference 44 and 47">(44, 47)</a> In addition, the carbon (doping and additives) in SIBs is known to contribute to SEI formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Interestingly, a survey of the literature (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) shows that high Na<sup>+</sup> uptake values are common in nanosized anatases during the first discharge irrespective of the electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub> or NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in different organic carbonate mixtures and even ionic liquids).</div><div class="NLM_p last">As the interest in SIBs is relatively recent, most of the studies (including anatase anodes) have been done using organic carbonate electrolytes. In fact, electrolyte optimization in SIBs is still in its infancy stages. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Organic carbonates raise safety concerns associated with flammability, volatility, and overall degradability above 50 °C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50-52)</a> Safety is a primary concern in stationary applications where large accumulation of storage/delivery components imposes more strict criteria for safety. As SIBs are intended for stationary applications, it seems rather logic to assume that safety concerns could be a limiting factor for the future applicability of SIBs. Aprotic ionic liquids, such as those based on pyrrolidinium, represent a safe alternative to organic carbonates in LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 50 and 53 to 56">(50, 53-56)</a> However, widespread use of ionic liquid electrolytes in batteries is currently hampered by slow diffusion of active ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 50, 51 and 53 to 57">(50, 51, 53-57)</a> Thus, current possible applications of ionic liquids in batteries must be limited to those in which power criteria can be relaxed in response to more strict safety criteria. As mentioned above, SIBs are intended for stationary applications, and in this direction, Na-ion-based ionic liquid electrolytes could certainly become competitive when compared to their Li-ion counterparts. Studies on ionic liquid electrolytes have been scarce. Up to our knowledge, there are only a few studies on the behavior of anatases in sodium metal half-cells with ionic liquids. Hagiwara et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> reported the high rate capability and good cycling performance of carbon-coated anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanopowders using [FSA]-[C<sub>3</sub>C<sub>1</sub>pyr][FSA] as electrolyte. However, working temperatures were around 90 °C (close to the melting point of Na metal). Perhaps, the more comparable study was that carried out with Pyr<sub>13</sub>FSI-NaFSI, which shows an acceptable performance at room temperature (RT). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> However, in that study, the characterization was limited to only one commercial sample, the carbon additive was set to 20 wt % (could contribute to SEI formation), and the specific capacity did not reach values during the first discharge well-beyond those of the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple (ca. 370 mAh/g or 1.1 Na<sup>+</sup>). Herein, we have taken benefit from the versatility of a self-assembly seeding-assisted method to obtain a variety of uniform high-surface-area undoped TiO<sub>2</sub> anatase nanostructures that differ in crystal size or nanostructure size. Electrodes built from these uniform nanostructures and with only a 10 wt % carbon additive were used as negative electrodes in sodium half-cells using Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI–NaTFSI as the ionic liquid electrolyte. Selection of this electrolyte was based on the fact that Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI is a relatively stable and affordable commercial ionic liquid, which also allows a direct comparison with similar nanostructures immersed in their Li-ion counterparts. Interestingly, the inherent low penetrability of the ionic liquid electrolyte has resulted in an unexpected asset to clarify large differences in Na<sup>+</sup> uptake by different nanostructures. Essentially, SEI effects were maximized and therefore clearly separated from electrochemical processes strictly associated with the anatase anodes.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Structural and Textural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S3</a> for a better visualization of high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) features) shows that all samples consist of uniform colloidal nanostructures built, according to HR-TEM, by more or less aligned anatase nanocrystals, exposing a majority of (101) surfaces. Recent studies on anatase anodes in SIBs have shown a dependence of the electrochemical response on the exposed surface. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Thus, exposing a majority of (101) surfaces assures excluding this factor from affecting the electrochemical activity. More specifically, the XRD for A6-50 shows peaks only corresponding to anatase (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>). The crystal size using the Scherrer equation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) gives a value of ca. 6 nm (similar to that inferred from the HR-TEM showed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>). According to scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and HR-TEM (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>), these anatase nanocrystals assemble into nanostructures with a uniform colloidal size of about 50 nm. Sample A11-50 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) basically differs from A6-50 in the anatase crystal size (ca. 11 vs 6 nm for A6-50). Sample A6-80 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>) differs from A6-50 in the nanostructure size (80 vs 50 nm). Here, we must mention that the protocol followed to prepare sample A6-50 was similar to the one described in ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">57</a>, although A6-50 was scaled up to produce ca. 2.5 g of nanostructures versus 1 g per batch. This scaling led to a slight increase in size, which is almost within the experimental error (50 vs 40 nm), and a more significant loss of mesocrystalline character (loss of alignment but importantly same (101) exposed surface). A careful look at the data also shows a slight decrease in the average nanostructure size from A6-50 (52 nm from size analysis) to A11-50 (48 nm from size analysis). This size reduction could reflect some densification during the different thermal treatment (250 °C/air/24 h vs 500 °C/N<sub>2</sub>/2 h), which is consistent with the reduction in the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area and the increase in the anatase crystal size. However, this difference is within both the experimental and precision errors and therefore cannot be considered as conclusive. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> summarizes the main structural and textural characteristics of the samples that were named as mentioned in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a> after the anatase crystal and nanostructure sizes for a better follow-up. Thus, A6-50 and A11-50 have a similar nanostructure size and a majority of (101) exposed surfaces, while differing in the anatase crystal size and BET surface area. Comparison of these two nanostructures could help us discern differences associated with changes in the anatase crystal size. On the other hand, A6-50 and A6-80 show a majority of (101) exposed surfaces, have a similar crystal size and BET surface area (importantly to N<sub>2</sub>), while differing in the nanostructure size. A comparison of the electrochemical behaviors of these two samples could, in principle, help us better understand a less explored factor (electrochemical activity as a function of nanostructure size or in general aggregate size effects). Indeed, A6-50 and A6-80 have similar N<sub>2</sub>-based BET surface areas, but this parameter does not assure similar penetrability of the electrolyte. Basically, in A6-80, the electrolyte has to diffuse a longer distance to be impregnated. Interestingly, this effect is maximized with ionic liquid electrolytes that show low penetrability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref57 ref58 ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 50 and 57 to 59">(50, 57-59)</a> The schematic representation displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> ideally captures the main differences between samples and their possible impact on the electrochemical activity.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (A) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns for all of the samples prepared here (A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80). The inset shows a zoom displaying the similar crystal sizes of the A6-50 and A6-80 samples, both differing from A11-50 (the largest). (B), (C), and (D) FE-SEM images for A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80, respectively. (E) Size histograms derived from the FE-SEM images. (F), (G), and (H) HR-TEM images at two different magnifications for A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80, respectively. The low-magnification HR-TEM pictures for the smallest nanostructures (A6-50 and A11-50) enable visualization of anatase planes and the full colloidal nanostructure in a single image (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S3</a> for a better visualization of all HR-TEM features). Basically, as better explained in the main text, all of the samples consist of uniform colloidal nanostructures formed by more or less aligned anatase nanocrystals, exposing a majority of (101) surfaces, differing in a controlled way in the anatase nanocrystal size and nanostructure size.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Schematic representation of the A11-50 (left), A6-50 (middle), and A6-80 (right) nanostructures, which ideally captures the main differences between the samples and their possible impact on the electrochemical activity. We must keep in mind that all nanostructures showed a majority of (101) exposed surfaces, which excludes this factor from being the cause of possible differences in the electrochemical activity.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Summary of the Structural and Textural Characteristics of the Different Nanostructures<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t1fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char"
