class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">145</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">140</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">30</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t1fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t1fn1"><sup>a</sup><p class="last">In addition to the BET surface area determined from N<sub>2</sub> isotherms, we also present the surface area values estimated from the nanostructure size and their corresponding anatase building blocks. The fact that BET areas are more similar to the ones derived from the anatase building blocks reflects the high accessibility of nanostructures to N<sub>2</sub>. Importantly, this high accessibility does not necessarily reflect the accessibility of the ionic liquid during electrochemical operation (wetting of all nanostructure volume).</p></div></div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Conductivity of the Pyr<sub>14</sub>-TFSI/NaTFSI (0.2 M) Electrolyte</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A limiting factor in battery performance with ionic liquid electrolytes is slow ion transport throughout the electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> The conductivity of the Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI/NaTFSI (0.2 M) electrolyte is similar to that of the LiTFSI counterpart at our working temperatures (30 and 60 °C) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). More specifically, the conductivity values for Na and Li electrolytes at 30 °C are 2.99 and 2.89 mS cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. At 60 °C, the conductivity values are 7.69 and 8.45 mS cm<sup>–1</sup>. Unlike in water and organic carbonates, the conductivity has important contributions from the solvent and therefore does not directly reveal the battery active ion conductivity. However, the similarity between Na and Li electrolytes suggests similar conductivities. Specifically, in our case, differences in ionic conductivities between both electrolytes, if any, may be attributable to only viscosity, which is strongly related to van der Waals interactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 60">(60)</a> In our case, the van der Waals interactions of both systems are similar, so even the ionic radii of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> being slightly different, we can expect similar values of conductivity. Basically, any significant difference between the electrochemical activities of Na-ion anatase anodes when compared to those of their Li-ion counterpart must be associated with other processes.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Impact of Nanostructure Features on the Formation of SEIs with Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI</h3><div class="NLM_p">As mentioned earlier, amorphization, disorder, and/or conversion clearly mark a departure point from the mechanism of Li-ion insertion in anatase. Also, as mentioned earlier, the activity of anatase electrodes takes place at potentials below 1 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, where SEIs can be formed, and SEIs in SIBs show different stability that that in their Li-ion counterparts. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref47" aria-label="reference 44 and 47">(44, 47)</a> In addition, ionic liquid electrolytes show SEIs of different nature to those of organic carbonate electrolytes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> and here, we are dealing with an unexplored liquid electrolyte (Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI). Carbon effects of relative importance in other studies can be discarded here, as we intentionally have limited its amount to only the 10 wt % coming from the conductive additive. Furthermore, our anodes are built upon well-defined undoped nanostructures with a relatively high surface area and a relatively small crystal size. High surface areas and small crystal sizes (surface species) are known to enhance SEI formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Thus, we carried out ex situ postmortem SEM studies to monitor the impact of nanostructure features on the formation of SEIs using Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI as electrolyte. These studies were carried out on electrodes cycled at 60 °C. At this temperature, electrolyte conductivity problems associated with poor ion transport throughout the electrolyte are minimized. Therefore, working at 60 °C assures a better electrochemical activity and a faster formation of the SEI. We must remember that we can proceed this way because ionic liquids are not flammable, have low vapor pressure, and in general efficiently operate at temperatures above 50 °C unlike organic carbonate electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Postmortem SEM images clearly capture the different impacts of nanostructures on SEI extension (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). Thus, SEM images confirm the high impact that A6-50 has on the formation of extended SEIs. Specifically, SEM pictures clearly reveal SEI domains that extend over 5 μm, severely blocking electrode porosity. These studies also reveal that SEI extension is larger for A6-80 than for A11-50 nanostructures. Specifically, for electrodes based on A6-80 nanostructures, the largest SEI domains extend over 500 nm, whereas for A11-50 nanostructures, the SEI (if exists) seems limited to scales smaller than the nanostructure itself (&lt;50 nm). Importantly, preservation of the initial nanostructure is clearly observed for A11-50. In any case, it seems clear that SEM studies anticipate a better performance of the A11-50 electrodes followed by A6-80 and A6-50.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (A), (B), and (C) Ex situ postmortem FE-SEM studies at different magnifications for electrodes built from A6-50, A6-80, and A11-50 nanostructures, respectively. SEM pictures clearly reveal more extended SEI domains for A6-50 that severely block electrode porosity (some SEI domains extend over 5 μm). For A6-80, SEI domains are significantly less extended (the largest SEI domains extend over 0.5 μm). Finally, electrodes built from A11-50 basically preserve their initial electrode conformation (SEI domains if exist ≪ 50 nm).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">SEM results indicate that the effect of nanostructures on the formation of SEIs strongly depends on size at different scale levels (nanocrystal and nanostructure). Thus, a change in the anatase crystal size from ca. 6 to 11 nm (A6-50 vs A11-50) strongly influences the extension of SEI domains. This result obviously could also be explained in terms of BET surface areas (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>), as usually is done when dealing with low- and high-surface-area carbons. Perhaps, more unexpected is the fact that samples with similar BET surface areas and anatase crystal sizes (A6-50 and A6-80) show pronounced differences. Basically, size effects seen through the nanostructure volume size strongly influence SEI formation. This result, basically, reminds us that care should be taken when comparing the electrochemical activity in terms of BET surface areas.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows the rate capability performance of the electrodes at 30 °C. As mentioned in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>, a working temperature of 30 °C was chosen instead of RT for a better thermostatic control with our controller. As described earlier, postmortem SEM studies indicated more extended SEI domains for anodes built from the A6-50 nanostructures (the largest SEI domains extended over 5 μm), which severely blocked electrode porosity. Therefore, we could expect the worst performance for this nanostructure, as indeed is observed in the rate capability results at 30 °C plotted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. Focusing on the A6-80 and A11-50 nanostructures, the latter shows a much better rate capability performance (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>). The postmortem SEM analysis carried out earlier clearly showed a more extended SEI for A6-80 (the largest SEI domains extended over 500 nm), which can also explain its worst performance when compared to that of A11-50. We must keep in mind that the inherent low penetrability of ionic liquid electrolytes could maximize SEI effects, allowing a clear separation of these processes from electrochemical processes strictly associated with the anatase anode. Thus, among the three nanostructures studied, A11-50 has the more effective combination of anatase crystal size and nanostructure size to favor the electrochemical activity while preventing the formation of extended SEIs.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical characterization of electrodes at 30 °C between 2.5 and 0.1 V. An applied current rate of 1 C corresponds to a specific current of 336 mA/g (based on the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple). (A) Rate capability performance of the three studied electrodes built upon the A6-50, A6-80, and A11-50 nanostructures. Current rates were changed after five cycles (0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 C). (B) First discharge curve for the three studied electrodes at a current rate of 0.2 C. (C), (D), and (E) Discharge–charge curves of the first five galvanostatic cycles at a current rate of 0.2 C for A6-50, A6-80, and A11-50, respectively. The inset in (C) clearly shows abrupt changes in the voltage decay during the first discharge of electrodes built from A6-50. (F) Cyclability performance at full discharge at a current rate of 0.5 C for the A11-50 nanostructures along with the corresponding Coulombic efficiency (Ceff) values (defined as the ratio of full capacity in charge/full capacity in discharge).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows the galvanostatic curves at 30 °C for the A6-50, A6-80, and A11-50 nanostructures at a current density of 0.2 C (67 mA/g). For electrodes built from the A6-50 nanostructures, the profile for the first discharge curve shows abrupt irregularities and, overall, the first five galvanostatic cycles manifest a poor performance. These curves clearly indicate that the extended SEIs observed during postmortem SEM studies block electrochemical processes in this electrode. Thus, this electrode was no further characterized, as the overall electrochemical response has a significant contribution from the extended SEI presence. In any case, although no further electrochemical analysis of this electrode was carried out, this result clearly reveal how SEIs can significantly slow down the electrochemical activity in Na-ion anatase electrodes. The voltage profile of A11-50 for the first discharge curve (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>) shows a sharp decrease from the open circuit voltage down to a voltage of ca. 0.5 V (uptake of 0.20 Na<sup>+</sup>), followed by a quasi-plateau region down to the cutoff voltage of 0.1 V (uptake of 0.85 Na<sup>+</sup>). This quasi-plateau, in a more detailed view, seems to consist of two separate regions with different slopes (even less pronounced at voltages about 0.2 V). Full explanation of these two different regions is given below during the characterization of this nanostructure at 60 °C, where these regions can be clearly separated (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> and detailed explanation below). The total uptake at 30 °C for the A11-50 electrodes (1.05) is close to 1 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula unit. For A6-80, the voltage profile shows basically a continuous decrease, corresponding to a total uptake of 0.60 Na<sup>+</sup>. Interestingly, in the upper voltage region (2.5–0.5 V) where both nanostructures show a similar profile (continuous curve), the Na<sup>+</sup> uptake is more significant for the A6-80 nanostructures (0.40 or 65% of total uptake) than that for A11-50 (0.20 or 20% of total uptake). In fact, the average potential for electrodes built from A6-80 is ca. 0.7 V, whereas for those built from A11-50, it is ca. 0.3 V. The upper voltage region includes the formation of SEIs with a possible contribution from pseudocapacitive processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref37" aria-label="reference 17, 18 and 37">(17, 18, 37)</a> However, the high irreversibility after the first cycle seems to downgrade the possible contribution of pseudocapacitive processes and point to the formation of SEIs. Thus, in A6-80, the overall mechanism seems to be controlled by the formation of a SEI, which prevents substantial uptake below 0.5 V (0.2 Na<sup>+</sup> vs 0.8–0.9 Na<sup>+</sup> for A11-50). This result confirms the conclusions drawn from postmortem SEM studies and supports the argument that the poor capability performance of A6-80, when compared to that of A11-50, is mainly associated with the formation of a more extended SEI. As SEI for A6-80 is not as extended as for A6-50, we remark again that the inherent low penetrability of ionic liquid electrolytes has maximized SEI effects, allowing a clear separation of these processes from electrochemical processes strictly associated with the anatase anode. As mentioned above, for A11-50, full explanation of the low-voltage region is given below during the characterization of this nanostructure at 60 °C, where different processes are clearly separated (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> and detailed explanation below).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical characterization of electrodes at 60 °C between 2.5 and 0.1 V. An applied current rate of 1 C corresponds to a specific current of 336 mA/g (based on the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple). (A) Discharge–charge curves of the first five galvanostatic cycles at a current rate of 0.2 C for A11-50. (B, C) Cyclic voltammograms at a scan rate of 0.05 mV/s for A11-50 and A6-80, respectively. (D) Rate capability performance of A11-50. Current rates were changed after five cycles (0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 C). (E) Cyclability performance at full discharge at a current rate of 0.5 C along with the corresponding Ceff values (defined as the ratio of full capacity in charge/full capacity in discharge) for A11-50.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After the first discharge process, the galvanostatic curves at 30 °C for A11-50 show a significant change in the voltage profile (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>). The voltage profiles do not show plateau regions, which is indicative of irreversible structural changes (including amorphization). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17, 18)</a> Thus, it seems that irreversibility in this nanostructure has a different origin to that detected for A6-80. Again, full explanation is given below during the characterization of this nanostructure at 60 °C, where results are better defined. The Ceff (first discharge–charge cycles) for A11-50 is 36%, whereas for the A6-80 electrode, it is 27%. These values are similar to those reported in the literature for TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes immersed in organic carbonate electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref25 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref25 ref41" aria-label="reference 7, 25 and 41">(7, 25, 41)</a> Interestingly, in the voltage curves of both electrodes, we do not observe the characteristic continuous decrease in the specific capacity after the first cycle. As better explained below after cyclability studies, this peculiar behavior can be explained in terms of the confluence of two opposite effects.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among the three nanostructures, A11-50 shows superior electrochemical response at 30 °C. Thus, we proceeded to further investigate its cyclability at 0.5 C (168 mAh/g). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows that the full specific capacity on discharge first decreases during the first five cycles and then increases until a value of about 100 mAh/g after 40 cycles. Finally, it reaches a relatively stable value at around 97 mAh/g (200 cycle). Capacity increases during the first cycles seem intrinsic to the uptake of Na<sup>+</sup> by anatases, as they have also been reported in organic carbonate electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref28" aria-label="reference 18, 19 and 28">(18, 19, 28)</a> This increase has been associated with a generic electrochemical activation process. In our system, the more peculiar behavior (decrease–increase–stability) can be explained in terms of (a) the irreversibility that prevails during the first cycles (decrease during first cycles); (b) the generic electrochemical activation process and/or better wetting that dominate the next cycles (increase); and (c) reaching the typical quasi-stationary regime. The Ceff (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>) follows a trend similar to that of specific capacity. It increases with the number of discharge/charge cycles, reaching a value of 99% after 200 cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">Safety being a primary concern in stationary applications, it seems reasonable to present the electrochemical activity at 60 °C for the electrodes showing the superior electrochemical performance at 30 °C (A11-50). Furthermore, as we explain below, this characterization has helped us to better understand the mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> uptake by anatase electrodes. Importantly, we must remember that the impact of nanostructures on SEI formation was checked at this temperature and showed excellent preservation of the initial morphology and no porosity blocking for A11-50 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). We must also keep in mind that high viscosity and slow ion transport effects are minimized for ionic liquids at 60 °C when compared to those at 30 °C, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> which anticipates a better electrochemical performance at 60 °C than at 30 °C. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> shows the galvanostatic curves at 60 °C for the electrode built from the A11-50 nanostructures at a current density of 0.2 C (67 mA/g). The shape of the first discharge curve slightly differs from the one at 30 °C. After a sharp drop from the open circuit voltage down to a voltage of ca. 0.7 V, we clearly see a quasi-plateau region down to 0.3 V corresponding to an uptake of ca. 0.8 Na<sup>+</sup>, followed at 0.3 V by a distinctive plateau corresponding to an uptake of ca. 0.5 Na<sup>+</sup>. Finally, we see a final decay down to the cutoff voltage of 0.1 V. Interestingly, during the charge cycle (desodiation process), the electrode recovers a capacity of 260 mAh/g, corresponding to a release of ca. 0.8 Na<sup>+</sup> (similar to the Na<sup>+</sup> uptake of the quasi-plateau region). Furthermore, during the subsequent cycles, the lower-voltage plateau region is no longer observed but the quasi-plateau region, though less defined, corresponds to a Na<sup>+</sup> uptake of ca. 0.6 Na<sup>+</sup>. The total uptake for the electrode built upon the A11-50 nanostructure during the first discharge (1.60 Na<sup>+</sup> or strictly 535 mAh/g as not all of this capacity has to be related to Na<sup>+</sup> uptake) is certainly higher than that corresponding to the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple. As mentioned earlier, values higher than those corresponding to ca. 1 Na<sup>+</sup> uptake per anatase formula are not rare and have been observed fundamentally in doped nanosized anatases (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref16 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref25 ref28 ref29 ref31 ref32 ref35 ref39 ref40 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref16 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref25 ref28 ref29 ref31 ref32 ref35 ref39 ref40 ref42" aria-label="reference 6, 16, 20 to 23, 25, 28, 29, 31, 32, 35, 39, 40 and 42">(6, 16, 20-23, 25, 28, 29, 31, 32, 35, 39, 40, 42)</a> Also, as mentioned earlier, for the A11-50 nanostructure, SEI seems to be limited to scales smaller than the nanostructure itself (&lt;50 nm). Furthermore, the electrode built upon A11-50 is basically an undoped anatase (only 10 wt % of carbon additive). Therefore, an uptake of 1.60 Na<sup>+</sup> (strictly 535 mAh/g as not all of this capacity has to be related to Na<sup>+</sup> uptake) could be understood in terms of first the insertion of ca. 1 Na<sup>+</sup> following the insertion mechanism proposed by Dambournet and co-workers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> The extra uptake of Na+ by the electrode (0.6 Na<sup>+</sup> or strictly 200 mAh/g as not all of this capacity has to be related with Na<sup>+</sup> uptake) could be associated with those processes of more irreversible nature than that associated with insertion. For instance, the conversion mechanism proposed by Passerini and co-workers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 and 18">(16, 18)</a> These findings are confirmed by analyzing the first four cyclic voltammograms at a scan rate of 0.05 mV/s for electrodes built from the A11-50 nanostructures (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). The cathodic sweep during the first cycle shows a reversible reduction peak at 0.6 V, followed by a strongly irreversible reduction peak at 0.2 V for the A11-50 nanostructures. For electrodes built from nanostructures with extended SEIs (A6-80), irreversibility clearly comes from SEI formation at high voltages. Thus, the cathodic sweep in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> displays an irreversible reduction peak at high potentials 1.9 V (not seen in A11-50) during the first cycle followed by a poorly defined reversible reduction peak at ca. 0.7 V (better resolved on the anodic sweeps). This reversible peak at ca. 0.7 V is similar though much lower in intensity than the reversible peak at 0.6 V observed for A11-50. Regarding the irreversible peak at 1.9 V, we must comment that the electrochemical activity above 1.5 V has been observed in Na-ion TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes immersed in organic carbonate electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref31" aria-label="reference 11 and 31">(11, 31)</a> A common characteristic is that these electrodes are built from high-surface-area samples. More specifically, a low-intensity peak at around 1.7 V was observed in the cyclic voltammograms of high-surface-area TiO<sub>2</sub> rutile mesocrystals immersed in ethylene/dimethyl carbonates and was generically assigned to SEI formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> The high-voltage irreversible peak at 1.9 V for electrodes based on A6-80, which is not detected for A11-50 electrodes, seems to corroborate its link to SEI reactions (confirmed by the postmortem SEM studies shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). Another important conclusion drawn from the comparison of voltammograms for A11-50 and A6-80 comes from the analysis of the lower-voltage region. The strongly irreversible peak at 0.2 V detected in the voltammogram of A11-50 is not detected for A6-80 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). As mentioned earlier, it seems clear that the formation of a SEI at high potentials for A6-80 prevents this process to take place.</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability performance for A11-50 is also displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. As expected, the performance is significantly better than that obtained at 30 °C. For example, at 5 C (1680 mAh/g), the specific capacity reaches values of 60 mAh/g vs. 20 mAh/g at 30 °C. As mentioned earlier, the high viscosity and slow ion transport effects are minimized for ionic liquids at 60 °C when compared to those at 30 °C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Finally, the cyclability performance at 0.5 C (168 mAh/g) is also shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. Interestingly, the trend after the fourth cycle in specific capacity values is close to the characteristic behavior observed in Li-ion anatase anodes (a decrease followed by a more or less quasi-stationary value). This behavior indicates that this sample at this temperature is electrochemically active almost from the beginning of the electrochemical process. Furthermore, the initial Ceff at this current is similar to the one at 30 °C (ca. 30%) and increases with the number of discharge/charge cycles, reaching a value close to 100% after 200 cycles. To summarize the results of the high-temperature study, if safety is a primary concern for stationary applications, the electrochemical response of the electrodes built upon the A11-50 nanostructures is excellent at 60 °C.</div><div class="NLM_p">Finally, the electrochemical activity of the optimized anatase nanostructure (A11-50) on Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI (0.2 M) was compared to that of similar nanostructures also in Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-LiTFSI (0.2 M). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> As mentioned earlier, SIBs are intended for replacement of LIBs in stationary applications. The results of that comparison in terms of the rate capability performance at both 30 and 60 °C are given in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>. Basically, the performances of Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI and Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-LiTFSI are similar at both temperatures, obviously, excluding the higher initial irreversibility value that is ubiquitous to Na-ion when compared to that of Li-ion anatase anodes. This, a priori, surprising result can be likely explained by the fact that the electrochemical response of anatase nanostructures in Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI is controlled (especially at high rates) by the poor ionic conductivity of the electrolyte. Finally, it seems important to mention that long-term cyclability studies were not compared because of the different stabilities of the Na and Li metal electrodes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> which does not assure a clean experiment.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Comparison of the rate capability performances at 30 and 60 °C for the best anatase nanostructure reported here (A11-50) in Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI (0.2 M) with a similar anatase nanostructure in Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-LiTFSI (also 0.2 M). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have found that uniform anatase nanostructures immersed in ionic liquid electrolytes (Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI) are excellent combinations to advance the understanding of Na-ion battery anatase anodes. Specifically, we have found that a controlled variation in the nanocrystal size and nanostructure size can lead to electrodes that have very different impacts on the electrolyte stability and that the inherent low penetrability of the ionic liquid electrolyte has resulted in an unexpected asset to clarify differences in Na<sup>+</sup> uptake (SEI effects maximized). Thus, comparison of electrodes with different impacts on SEI formation has indicated that during the first discharge the process associated with a more or less defined plateau at ca. 0.3 V stops from being operative when SEIs block electrode porosity. For the well-preserved electrodes, Na<sup>+</sup> uptakes well-beyond those of the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox couple during the first cycle have been explained in terms of a combination of two mechanisms with different degrees of reversibility. Our results emphasize that any conclusions on mechanistic studies, other than size effects, must be done under strict control on size at various scales and taking into consideration SEI presence. Furthermore, the fact that electrodes built from nanostructures with similar crystal sizes and BET surface areas but different nanostructure sizes (size effects seen through the nanostructure volume size) differ in their impact toward SEI formation reminds us that care should be taken when comparing the electrochemical activity in terms of BET surface areas. Finally, we have found that at 30 and 60 °C the performance of the best of the electrodes with the ionic liquid electrolyte is comparable to that of similar nanostructures immersed in their Li-ion counterparts. This result is somehow promising, as in stationary applications where SIBs could replace LIBs, large accumulation of storage units turn safety into an even more demanding priority. Basically, power criteria can be relaxed in response to more strict safety criteria.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Anatase Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">We have previously reported that a method that combines thermally driven self-assembly of nanomicelles with seeding-assisted chemistry allows the preparation of mesoporous anatase nanostructures with a uniform colloidal size and good textural properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref61 ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 57, 61 and 62">(57, 61, 62)</a> These nanostructures can be described as colloidal structures of more or less oriented anatase nanocrystals (more or less mesocrystalline character) exposing a majority of (101) surfaces. Here, we have used this methodology for the preparation of a variety of anatase nanostructures that differ in the size of the anatase nanocrystal units and nanostructure size. Specifically, we have prepared three different samples for this study named as A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80. As we have shown in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Results and Discussion">Results and Discussion</a> section (samples characterization), these names allow a rapid identification of the samples based on the anatase crystal size and the nanostructure size. For the preparation of nanostructure A6-50, we have followed the same protocol as that reported earlier by some of us. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> However, here, we have scaled up the process 2.5 times (ca. 2.5 vs 1 g of nanostructures per batch for that study). For the preparation of nanostructure A11-50, we have followed a similar protocol to that carried out for A6-50, although heating in air at 250 °C/24 h was replaced by heating in a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at 500 °C for 2 h. Finally, nanostructure A6-80 was obtained by reducing a 30 wt % the amount of seeds added during the initial synthesis. Briefly, the procedure to obtain the nanostructures consisted in preparing a microemulsion containing the anatase precursors by dissolving TiOSO<sub>4</sub> (1.25 M in H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) solutions in an organic component (cyclohexane) with the help of a nonionic surfactant (Igepal CO-520), followed by the addition of anatase nanoseeds and a two-stage solvothermal treatment at 60 and 80 °C, washing, drying, and a final thermal treatment. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> summarizes the specifics of the different reagents and specific protocols used for the preparation of these samples.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Electrolyte Preparation and Conductivity Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The ionic liquid electrolyte was prepared by dissolving 0.2 M sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI) in <i>N</i>-methyl-(<i>n</i>-butyl)pyrrolidinium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl) imide (Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI from MERCK). Dielectric spectroscopy (using a Novocontrol Alpha analyzer temperature controller over the frequency range 0.1 Hz–10 MHz with an ac perturbation of 10 mV and stabilization time of 10 min) was used to measure the ionic conductivity of the Pyr<sub>14</sub>TFSI-NaTFSI (0.2 M) electrolyte as a function of temperature as well as their Li counterparts. The ionic conductivity was extracted from plotting the real and imaginary parts of the complex impedance using ZPlot fitting software. Importantly, in the specific case of pyrrolidinium-based ionic liquids, the conductivity decreases with the increase in the active ion concentration. To select the adequate concentration of NaTFSI, a compromise between Na<sup>+</sup> concentration and conductivity must be achieved (0.2 M is an adequate value).</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Structural and Textural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">XRD patterns were recorded on a Bruker D8 Advance instrument (Cu Kα radiation, 40 kV, 30 mA). The crystal domain size was determined from the X-ray peak profiles using the Scherrer equation applied to the (200) diffraction peak. The morphology was investigated by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE–SEM, Hitachi, SU 8000). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images were taken in a JEOL 300 kV field emission microscope. To determine the nanostructure size distribution, several micrographs were taken for each sample in different regions of the holder and around 100 particles were manually measured using Adobe Photoshop. BET surface areas were obtained from the analysis of the N<sub>2</sub> isotherm at low pressures (0.05–0.20).</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">For the electrochemical characterization, film electrodes were prepared having a final composition of 80 wt % TiO<sub>2</sub> anatase nanostructures, 10 wt % conductive carbon (TIMREX Super-P), and 10 wt % poly(vinylidene fluoride) binder (<i>M</i><sub>w</sub> ∼534 000, Aldrich). For film preparation, all of the components were first dispersed in 1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (Aldrich). The obtained slurry was then stirred for 24 h and casted on a copper foil (current collector) using a doctor blade. Film electrodes were dried at 70 °C for 1.5 h and then at 120 °C under vacuum, overnight. Finally, the dried electrodes were transferred to an argon glove box (H<sub>2</sub>O content &lt;1 ppm) for cell assembly. The active material mass loading was approximately 1.0 mg.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Coin-type cell configurations (size 2032) were assembled in an argon glove box. A sheet of Whatman BSF-80 glass fiber was used as a separator, which was drenched with an electrolyte. Sodium metal (sticks pure, PanReac AppliChem) was used as the counter and reference electrodes. Coin cells were maintained at 60 °C for 12 h prior to any electrochemical measurement to assure better wetting. Galvanostatic curves were registered with an Arbin-BT4 battery system, while a VMP3 Potentiostat (BILOGIC) was used to record cyclic voltammetry curves. All electrochemical studies were performed within a cell voltage window between 2.5 and 0.1 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. It should be noted that all electrochemical measurements were duplicated to minimize doubts about the reliability of the measurement. Electrochemical studies were carried out at two different temperatures (30 and 60 °C) under thermostatic conditions at different temperatures. Importantly, a working temperature of 30 °C was chosen instead of RT for a better thermostatic control.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsomega.7b00548</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Table S1: summary of previous studies on Na-ion anatase electrodes; Table S2: amounts of reagents and protocol used for preparation of nanostructures; Figures S1–S3: high magnification images of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>; and Figure S4: conductivity measurements of ionic liquid electrolytes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf">ao7b00548_si_001.pdf (1.98 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsomega.7b00548" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review—Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research on electrochem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries.  In this article, practical issues and perspective are reviewed on the basis of mainly authors exptl. experiences, know-how, and results, and the future direction is proposed to overcome the issues and to challenge the advanced performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.10.033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Longoni, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J. M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ruffo, R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Na2FeP2O7-Carbon Nanotubes Composite as High Rate Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                 10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.10.033</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D302%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D61-69%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DG.%2BLongoni%26author%3DJ.%2BE.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BH.%2BJung%26author%3DD.%2BK.%2BKim%26author%3DC.%2BM.%2BMari%26author%3DR.%2BRuffo%26title%3DThe%2BNa2FeP2O7-Carbon%2BNanotubes%2BComposite%2Bas%2BHigh%2BRate%2BCathode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2015.10.033&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00548&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2015.10.033&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Na2FeP2O7-carbon nanotubes composite as high rate cathode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Longoni, Gianluca; Wang, Ji Eun; Jung, Young Hwa; Kim, Do Kyung; Mari, Claudio M.; Ruffo, Riccardo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">302</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">61-69</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Among the viable pos. electrode materials recently proposed for Na-ion secondary batteries, Na2FeP2O7 was investigated thanks to its facile prepn., the use of highly abundant and low cost raw materials, and the highest thermal stability among all others cathode materials.  In the present work the electrochem. features of the Na2FeP2O7 are improved by synthesizing a Na2FeP2O7-carbon nanotubes composite with prominent high-rate performances.  The material shows a reversible specific capacity of 86 mA h g-1 for 140 cycles at 1C and 68 mA h g-1 at 10C.  An in depth investigation about the Na+ diffusion rates inside the material was conducted by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/nl500548a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van Aken, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries are one of the realistic promising alternatives to the Li analogs.  However, neither theor. energy/power d. nor the practical values reach the values of Li cathodes.  Poorer performance is expected owing to larger size, larger mass, and lower cell voltage.  Nonetheless, Na ion batteries are considered to be practically relevant in view of the abundance of the element Na.  The arguments in favor of Li and to the disadvantage of Na would be completely obsolete if the specific performance data of the latter would match the 1st.  Here the authors present a cathode consisting of C-coated nanosized Na3V2(PO4)3 embedded in a porous C matrix, which not only matches but even out-shines Li cathodes under high rate conditions.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase TiO2: Better Anode Material Than Amorphous and Rutile Phases of TiO2 for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Dawei; Dou, Shixue; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous TiO2@C nanospheres were synthesized via a template approach.  After being sintered under different conditions, two types of polyphase TiO2 hollow nanospheres were obtained.  The electrochem. properties of the amorphous TiO2 nanospheres and the TiO2 hollow nanospheres with different phases were characterized as anodes for the Na-ion batteries.  It was found that all the samples demonstrated excellent cyclability, which was sustainable for hundreds of cycles with little capacity fading, although the anatase TiO2 presented a capability that was better than that of the mixed anatase/rutile TiO2 or the amorphous TiO2@C.  Through crystallog. anal., it was revealed that the anatase TiO2 crystal structure supplies two-dimensional diffusion paths for Na-ion intercalation and more accommodation sites.  D. functional theory calcns. indicated lower energy barriers for the insertion of Na+ into anatase TiO2.  Therefore, anatase TiO2 hollow nanospheres show excellent high-rate performance.  Through ex situ field emission SEM, it was revealed that the TiO2 hollow nanosphere architecture can be maintained for hundreds of cycles, which is the main reason for its superior cyclability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/c3cc45254a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Memarzadeh Lotfabad, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Farbod, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kohandehghan, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mitlin, D.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanocrystalline Anatase TiO2: A New Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Exploring new anode materials is important for developing sodium ion batteries.  In this work, TiO2(B) nanotubes are prepd. by hydrothermal reaction in concd. NaOH soln. and subsequent calcination at 300 °C in air, and investigated for the first time as anodes for sodium ion batteries.  It is demonstrated that TiO2(B) nanotubes are several hundred nanometers in length with an outer diam. of about 10-15 nm.  Moreover, the interference fringe spacings of the nanotubes are derived from the TEM image, being about 0.56 and 0.34 nm, which are consistent with the interplanar distances of the (001) and (110) planes in the TiO2(B) phase, resp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchical tubular structures constructed from rutile TiO2 nanorods are fabricated, using MnO2 nanorods as sacrificial templates. This type of hierarchical structures with high porosity produced by neighboring building blocks, are desirable for the development of sodium ion batteries, because the large amts. of pores can enhance the contact areas between electrode and electrolyte, and faciliate the ion diffusion during the charge/discharge process.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-organized amorphous TiO2 nanotube arrays (NTAs) were successfully fabricated on both Ti foil and porous Ti foam through electrochem. anodization techniques.  The starting Ti foams were fabricated using ARCAM's Electron Beam Melting (EBM) technol.  The TiO2 NTAs on Ti foam were used as anodes in lithium ion batteries; they exhibited high capacities of 103 μAh cm-2 at 10 μA cm-2 and 83 μAh cm-2 at 500 μA cm-2, which are two to three times higher than those achieved on the std. Ti foil, which is around 40 μAh cm-2 at 10 μA cm-2 and 24 μAh cm-2 at 500 μA cm-2, resp.  This improvement is mainly attributed to higher surface area of the Ti foam and higher porosity of the nanotube arrays layer grown on the Ti foam.  In addn., a Na-ion half-cell composed of these NTAs anodes and Na metal as the counter electrode showed a self-improved specific capacity upon cycling at 10 μA cm-2.  These results indicate that TiO2 NTAs grown on Ti porous foam are promising electrodes for Li-ion or Na-ion rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1021/jz2012066"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiong, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Amorphous TiO2 Nanotube Anode for Rechargeable Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiong, Hui; Slater, Michael D.; Balasubramanian, Mahalingam; Johnson, Christopher S.; Rajh, Tijana</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are an attractive alternative to lithium-ion batteries that alleviate problems with lithium availability and cost.  Despite several studies of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries involving layered oxide materials, there are few low-voltage metal oxide anodes capable of operating sodium ion reversibly at room temp.  We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  We find that only amorphous large diam. nanotubes (&gt;80 nm I.D.) can support electrochem. cycling with sodium ions.  These electrodes maximize their capacity in operando and reach reversible capacity of 150 mA-h/g in 15 cycles.  We also demonstrate for the first time a full cell all-oxide sodium-ion battery using amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube anode coupled to a Na1.0Li0.2Ni0.25Mn0.75Oδ cathode at room temp. exhibiting good rate capability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501489"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tahir, M.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Extraordinary Performance of Carbon-Coated Anatase TiO2 as Sodium-Ion Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tahir Muhammad Nawaz; Panthofer Martin; Tremel Wolfgang; Oschmann Bernd; Buchholz Daniel; Dou Xinwei; Passerini Stefano; Lieberwirth Ingo; Zentel Rudolf</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced energy materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of in situ polymer-functionalized anatase TiO2 particles using an anchoring block copolymer with hydroxamate as coordinating species is reported, which yields nanoparticles (≈11 nm) in multigram scale.  Thermal annealing converts the polymer brushes into a uniform and homogeneous carbon coating as proven by high resolution transmission electron microscopy and Raman spectroscopy.  The strong impact of particle size as well as carbon coating on the electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 is demonstrated.  Downsizing the particles leads to higher reversible uptake/release of sodium cations per formula unit TiO2 (e.g., 0.72 eq.  Na(+) (11 nm) vs only 0.56 eq.  Na(+) (40 nm)) while the carbon coating improves rate performance.  The combination of small particle size and homogeneous carbon coating allows for the excellent electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 at high (134 mAh g(-1) at 10 C (3.35 A g(-1))) and low (≈227 mAh g(-1) at 0.1 C) current rates, high cycling stability (full capacity retention between 2nd and 300th cycle at 1 C) and improved coulombic efficiency (≈99.8%).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b00098"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fukunishi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Morgan, B.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase TiO2 nanoparticles for high power sodium-ion anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Liming; Buchholz, Daniel; Bresser, Dominic; Gomes Chagas, Luciana; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">251</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">379-385</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be a promising low-cost alternative to common lithium-ion batteries.  Herein, we present a detailed electrochem. characterization of anatase TiO2 nanoparticles as anode material for Na-ion batteries, highlighting the substantial influence of the electrolyte compn. (salt and solvent) on the obtainable specific capacity, cycling stability, and particularly the coulombic efficiency.  TiO2-based electrodes cycled using a 1M soln. of NaClO4 in a mixt. of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate present excellent high rate capability, offering about 100 and 86 mAh g-1 at 5.5 and 11 C, resp., which is - to the best of our knowledge - the best high rate capability for all titanium-based sodium-ion anode materials reported so far.  Setting the C rate to 5.5C (i.e., 1.85 A g-1), such electrodes can be cycled for more than 1000 cycles without significant capacity decay, confirming their outstanding durability at such high specific current.  These results, in combination with its environmental friendliness and cost efficiency, render anatase TiO2 nanoparticles a promising anode material for high power sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1021/nl402747x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, K.-T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ali, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chung, K.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase Titania Nanorods as an Intercalation Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Ki-Tae; Ali, Ghulam; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Yoon, Chong Seung; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Sun, Yang-Kook; Lu, Jun; Amine, Khalil; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">416-422</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V.  With the help of the conductive carbon layers, the carbon-coated anatase TiO2 delivers more capacity at high rates, 104 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g), 82 mA-h/g at the 30C rate (10 A/g), and 53 mA-h/g at the 100C rate (33 A g-1).  By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).  The excellent cyclability and high-rate capability are the result of a Na+ insertion and extn. reaction into the host structure coupled with Ti4+/3+ redox reaction, as shown by x-ray absorption spectroscopy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1149/2.0031502jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bresser, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oschmann, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tahir, M.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nitrogen-doped open pore channeled graphene facilitating electrochemical performance of TiO2 nanoparticles as an anode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cha, Hyun Ae; Jeong, Hyung Mo; Kang, Jeung Ku</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report that titanium dioxide nanoparticles anchored on nitrogen-doped open pore channeled graphene exhibit high performance as anode materials for sodium ion batteries with a high reversible capacity of 405 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1, excellent cycle stability with a capacity of 250 mA h g-1 over 100 charge-discharge cycles at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1, and superior rate capability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">N doped graphene supported TiO2 hollow nanostructures varying from 10 to 15 nm were designed for Na ion battery anode applications, which were obtained by a 2-step technique hydrothermal-calcining process with urea as an inhibitor and a N source. The large sp. surface area is beneficial for better contact between the electrode materials and the electrolyte.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the merits of good chem. stability, elemental abundance and nontoxicity, TiO2 (TiO2) has drawn increasing attraction for use as anode material in Na-ion batteries.  Nanostructured TiO2 was able to achieve high energy d.  However, nanosized TiO2 is typically electrochem. instable, which leads to poor cycling performance.  To improve the cycling stability, C from thermolysis of poly(vinyl pyrrolidone) was coated onto TiO2 nanoparticles.  Electronic cond. and electrochem. stability were enhanced by coating C onto TiO2 nanoparticles.  The resultant C-coated TiO2 nanoparticles exhibited high reversible capacity (242.3 mAh g-1), high coulombic efficiency (97.8%), and good capacity retention (87.0%) at 30 mA g-1 over 100 cycles. The rate performance of C-coated TiO2 nanoparticles was also displayed ≤127.6 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 800 mA g-1.  The improved cycling performance and rate capability were mostly attributed to protective C layer helping stabilize solid electrolyte interface formation of TiO2 nanoparticles and improving the electronic cond.  Therefore, C-coated TiO2 nanoparticles are promising anode candidate for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2015.08.051"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Du, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">TiO2/carbon Hollow Spheres as Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim. The structural and morphol. characterizations reveal that the TiO2@rGO composite has a sphere-like morphol. with diams. of 5-15 μm, and each microsphere is actually a random aggregation of primary TiO2 nanoparticles, which are tightly covered by soft graphene sheets.  The TiO2@rGO electrode exhibits enhanced electrochem. performance of high charge capacity (170 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C), long cycling life (87% of capacity retention after 300 cycles at 0.2 C), and high rate capability (55 mA h g-1 at 10 C).                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultrafast sodium storage in anatase TiO2 nanoparticles embedded on carbon nanotubes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Lee, Joo-Hyeong; Abouimrane, Ali; Belharouak, Ilias; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">218-226</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The main disadvantage of using transition metal oxides for Na+-ion batteries is the sluggish kinetics of insertion of Na+ ions into the structure.  Here, we introduce nanosized anatase TiO2 that is partially doped with fluorine (TiO2-δFδ) to form electro-conducting trivalent Ti3+ as an ultrafast Na+ insertion material for use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  In addn., the F-doped TiO2-δFδ is modified by electro-conducting carbon nanotubes (CNTs) to further enhance the elec. cond.  The composite F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is produced in a one-pot hydrothermal reaction.  X-ray diffraction and microscopic studies revealed that nanocryst. anatase-type TiO2-δFδ particles, in which fluorine is present with TiO2 particles, are loaded on the CNTs.  This yields a high elec. cond. of approx. 5.8 S cm-1.  The first discharge capacity of the F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is approx. 250 mA h (g-oxide)-1, and is retained at 97% after 100 cycles.  As expected, a high-rate performance was achieved even at the 100 C discharging rate (25 A g-1) where the composite material demonstrated a capacity of 118 mA h g-1 under the 0.1 C-rate charge condition.  The present work also highlights a significant improvement in the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions when the material was charged and discharged under the same rate of 35 C (8.75 A g-1), delivering approx. 90 mA h (g-oxide)-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA08472A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cai, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nitrogen-Doped TiO 2 Nanospheres for Advanced Sodium-Ion Battery and Sodium-Ion Capacitor Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nitrogen-doped TiO2 nanospheres for advanced sodium-ion battery and sodium-ion capacitor applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Sainan; Cai, Zhenyang; Zhou, Jiang; Pan, Anqiang; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) and sodium-ion capacitors (NICs) are considered to be promising energy storage systems for applications in future hybrid elec. vehicles (HEVs) and elec. vehicles (EVs) because of the low cost and abundance of Na.  Herein, NIBs and NICs based on nitrogen-doped TiO2 (referred to as N-TiO2) nanospheres as anode materials were analyzed.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-Assembled Synthesis of Mesocrystalline TiO2@C-rGO Hybrid Nanostructures for Highly Reversible Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Zhensheng; Zhou, Kaiqiang; Zhang, Junwen; Huang, Zhigao; Wei, Mingdeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Crystal Growth &amp; Design</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6605-6612</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CGDEFU</span>;
