            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the aim of enhancing the electrochem. kinetics and capacity of the TiO2 electrode for Na ion batteries (NIBs), we have designed a hybrid material of carbon-coated TiO2 mesocrystals anchored on reduced graphene oxide (TiO2@C-rGO).  Such hybrid nanostructures are fabricated through a facile one-step route including in situ growth of oriented self-assembly of TiO2 mesocrystals on GO.  TiO2@C-rGO possesses a very large surface area (279 m2 g-1), mesoporous nature, and single-crystal-like structure.  It is also found that the capacity of TiO2 electrode for NIBs could be improved by carbon coating at a low current rate, but pure TiO2 shows better rate performance than that of TiO2@C.  Remarkably, the enhanced electrochem. kinetics and large capacity can be simultaneously achieved by designing hybrid material.  The hybrid nanostructures exhibit a highly reversible capacity of 300 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, superior rate capability, and long-term cycling stability (a stable capacity of 159 mAh g-1 can be maintained after 1000 cycles at 1 A g-1).  The superior Na ion storage of TiO2@C-rGO is largely ascribed to the robust architecture of well-dispersed carbon-coated mesoporous TiO2 mesocrystals anchored on conductive graphene network, leading to enhanced electrochem. kinetics and offering enough active sites for the Na ion to locate.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.06.150"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tan, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-Pot Synthesis of Bicrystalline Titanium Dioxide Spheres with a Core–shell Structure as Anode Materials for Lithium and Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase TiO2 nanocubes for fast and durable sodium ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Xuming; Wang, Chao; Yang, Yingchang; Zhang, Yan; Jia, Xinnan; Chen, Jun; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8800-8807</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the aim of advancing anatase TiO2 anodes for Na ion batteries, cryst. TiO2 nanocubes were employed and they delivered a gradually increasing capacity during the initial cycles, termed as an activation process.  The no. of necessary discharge-charge loops for total activation is dependent on the galvanostatic c.d. (∼20 cycles at 0.2 C, or 90 cycles at 1 C).  A percentage of Ti3+ was detected after the activation, indicating an amt. of irreversibly trapped Na ions in the lattice.  After the activation process, an excellent rate capability and outstanding cycling stability were presented.  The reversible capacity reached 174, 132, and 108 mA h g-1 at rates of 1 C, 5 C, and 10 C, resp.  The capacity was sustained with a loss of &lt;10% after 1000 discharge-charge cycles at a rate of 2 C or 10 C.  The superior battery performance achieved by the nanocubes is related to the encircled {100} facets that are more favorable for Na ion attachment compared to the {001} and {101} facets, as supported by 1st-principles calcns.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Combining ionic liquid-based electrolytes and nanostructured anatase TiO2 anodes for intrinsically safer sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Liming; Moretti, Arianna; Buchholz, Daniel; Passerini, Stefano; Bresser, Dominic</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In an attempt to realize Na-ion batteries with enhanced safety, the authors report herein the use of ionic liq. (IL)-based electrolytes for cycling nanoparticulate anatase TiO2 as Na-ion anode material.  The use of the IL-based electrolyte results in the highly stable cycling performance of anatase TiO2-based electrodes, providing an initial specific capacity of 159 mAh g-1 when applying a specific current of 33.5 mA g-1, and still 155 mAh g-1 after 80 full (dis-)charge cycles.  Also, a very promising rate performance was obtained with specific capacities of 108 and 78 mAh g-1 for specific currents of 335 and 670 mA g-1, resp.  Indeed, the excellent electrochem. performance, and high coulombic efficiency, as well as the electrochem. impedance spectroscopy results indicate the highly beneficial impact of the IL-based electrolyte on the formation of a stabilized solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00491"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mogensen, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Brandell, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Younesi, R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Solubility of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase (SEI) in Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Energy Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">1</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1173</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1178</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solubility of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase (SEI) in Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mogensen, Ronnie; Brandell, Daniel; Younesi, Reza</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is often stated that formation of a functional solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) in sodium ion batteries is hampered by the higher soly. of SEI components such as sodium salts in comparison to the lithium analogs.  In order to investigate these phenomena, SEI formation and functionality, as well as cell self-discharge, are studied for the sodium ion system with comparative expts. on the equiv. lithium ion system.  By conducting a set of expts. on carbonaceous anodes, the impact of SEI dissoln. is tested.  The results show that the SEI layer in sodium ion cells is inferior to that in lithium ion counterparts with regards to self-discharge; sodium cells show a loss in capacity at a dramatic rate as compared to the lithium counterparts when they are stored at sodiated and lithiated states, resp., for a long time with no external applied current or potential.  Also, synchrotron-based hard XPS measurements indicate that the major factor leading to increased self-discharge is dissoln. of significant parts of the sodium-based SEI.  Furthermore, the effect of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) electrolyte additive on self-discharge is tested as part of the work.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1039/c4cp00826j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tokiwa, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Negative Electrodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comparative study of the electrode/electrolyte interface was carried out for lithium and sodium metal anodes in electrolytes consisting in 1 M LiPF6 in EC0.5:DMC0.5 (LP30) and 1 M NaPF6 in both EC0.5:DMC0.5 and EC0.45PC0.45DMC0.1. In contrast, large overpotentials were obsd. even at 0.1 mA/cm2 for Na/Na cells.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The 1st review of the various electrolytes currently used and developed for Na-ion batteries (SIBs), both in terms of materials and concepts, is presented.  In contrast to the Li-ion battery (LIB), which is a mature technol. for which a more or less unanimously accepted std. electrolyte exists: 1 M LiPF6 in EC/DMC, the electrolyte of choice for SIBs has not yet fully conformed to a std.  This is true for both materials: salts, solvents, or additives, and concept, using the main track of org. solvents or aiming for other concepts.  SIB research currently prospers, benefitting from using know-how gained from 30 years of LIB R and D.  Here the currently employed electrolytes are emphasized and their effects on practical SIB performance are outlined, scrutinizing the rationale for specific choices made, salts, solvents, additives, concns., etc. for each specific cell set-up and usage conditions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref50" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2010.09.080"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The paper reviews properties of room temp. ionic liqs. (RTILs) as electrolytes for lithium and lithium-ion batteries.  It has been shown that the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the anode surface is crit. to the correct operation of secondary lithium-ion batteries, including those working with ionic liqs. as electrolytes.  The SEI layer may be formed by electrochem. transformation of (i) a mol. additive, (ii) RTIL cations or (iii) RTIL anions.  Such properties of RTIL electrolytes as viscosity, cond., vapor pressure and lithium-ion transport nos. are also discussed from the point of view of their influence on battery performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref56" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.03.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nohira, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kuroda, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hagiwara, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fukunaga, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sakai, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nitta, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Inazawa, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NaFSA–C1C3pyrFSA Ionic Liquids for Sodium Secondary Battery Operating over a Wide Temperature Range</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA ionic liquids for sodium secondary battery operating over a wide temperature range</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Changsheng; Nohira, Toshiyuki; Kuroda, Keisuke; Hagiwara, Rika; Fukunaga, Atsushi; Sakai, Shoichiro; Nitta, Koji; Inazawa, Shinji</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The physicochem. properties of NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA ionic liqs. were investigated to explore their potential as new electrolytes for sodium secondary batteries operating over a wide temp. range.  The viscosity and ionic cond. of NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA with a molar ratio of 2:8 were 16.7 cP and 15.6 mS cm-1, resp., at 353 K.  The electrochem. window of this ionic liq. was 5.2 V at 353 K with sodium metal deposition detg. the cathode limit.  A Na/NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA/NaCrO2 cell exhibited stable charge-discharge behavior at 298 and 353 K.  The discharge capacities of 92 and 106 mAh (g-NaCrO2)-1 were obsd. at 298 and 353 K, resp., at 20 mA g-1.  The Coulombic efficiency was higher than 99% during the charge-discharge tests except for the initial few cycles.  The cell also showed high discharge rates, reaching 500 mA g-1 at 353 K.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref57" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.09.106"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Amarilla, J. In particular, we have studied ternary mixts. based on the lithium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) salt and two ionic liqs. sharing the same cation (N-methyl-N-Pr pyrrolidinium, PYR13), but different anions, bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (TFSI) and bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (FSI). data-id="article_drawer_TabFigures" aria-selected="true" class="active" aria-label="Figures"><i class="icon-image"></i><span>Figures</span></a></li><li role="presentation"><a id="pane-m2con" href="#pane-m2" aria-controls="pane-m2" role="tab" data-toggle="tab" data-id="article_drawer_TabReferences" title="References" aria-selected="false" class="references-tab"><i data-id="article_drawer_TabSupportingInfo" title="Supporting Info" aria-selected="false" class="tab__nav-suppInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Info</span></a></li></ul><div class="paneContainer"><ul class="rlist tab__content" tabindex="0"><li id="pane-m1" aria-labelledby="pane-m1" class="tab__pane tab__pane-figures active"><figure data-id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Abstract</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#f_tgr1"><img class="rightTab-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0002.gif" alt="" id="rightTab-tgr1"></a><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0007.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr1" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (A) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns for all of the samples prepared here (A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80). The inset shows a zoom displaying the similar crystal sizes of the A6-50 and A6-80 samples, both differing from A11-50 (the largest). (B), (C), and (D) FE-SEM images for A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80, respectively. (E) Size histograms derived from the FE-SEM images. (F), (G), and (H) HR-TEM images at two different magnifications for A6-50, A11-50, and A6-80, respectively. The low-magnification HR-TEM pictures for the smallest nanostructures (A6-50 and A11-50) enable visualization of anatase planes and the full colloidal nanostructure in a single image (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/suppl_file/ao7b00548_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S3</a> for a better visualization of all HR-TEM features). Basically, as better explained in the main text, all of the samples consist of uniform colloidal nanostructures formed by more or less aligned anatase nanocrystals, exposing a majority of (101) surfaces, differing in a controlled way in the anatase nanocrystal size and nanostructure size.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Schematic representation of the A11-50 (left), A6-50 (middle), and A6-80 (right) nanostructures, which ideally captures the main differences between the samples and their possible impact on the electrochemical activity. We must keep in mind that all nanostructures showed a majority of (101) exposed surfaces, which excludes this factor from being the cause of possible differences in the electrochemical activity.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0001.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (A), (B), and (C) Ex situ postmortem FE-SEM studies at different magnifications for electrodes built from A6-50, A6-80, and A11-50 nanostructures, respectively. SEM pictures clearly reveal more extended SEI domains for A6-50 that severely block electrode porosity (some SEI domains extend over 5 μm). For A6-80, SEI domains are significantly less extended (the largest SEI domains extend over 0.5 μm). Finally, electrodes built from A11-50 basically preserve their initial electrode conformation (SEI domains if exist ≪ 50 nm).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00548&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-005482_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00548/asset/images/large/ao-2017-005482_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review—Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research on electrochem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries.  In this article, practical issues and perspective are reviewed on the basis of mainly authors exptl. experiences, know-how, and results, and the future direction is proposed to overcome the issues and to challenge the advanced performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.10.033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Longoni, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries are one of the realistic promising alternatives to the Li analogs.  However, neither theor. energy/power d. nor the practical values reach the values of Li cathodes.  Poorer performance is expected owing to larger size, larger mass, and lower cell voltage.  Nonetheless, Na ion batteries are considered to be practically relevant in view of the abundance of the element Na.  The arguments in favor of Li and to the disadvantage of Na would be completely obsolete if the specific performance data of the latter would match the 1st.  Here the authors present a cathode consisting of C-coated nanosized Na3V2(PO4)3 embedded in a porous C matrix, which not only matches but even out-shines Li cathodes under high rate conditions.  It can be (dis)charged in 6 s with a c.d. ≤22 A/g (200 C), still delivering a specific capacity of 44 mA-h/g, while up to 20 C, the polarization is completely negligible.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b04347"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Longoni, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pena Cabrera, R.                         rightTab-cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase TiO2: Better Anode Material Than Amorphous and Rutile Phases of TiO2 for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Dawei; Dou, Shixue; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6022-6029</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous TiO2@C nanospheres were synthesized via a template approach.  After being sintered under different conditions, two types of polyphase TiO2 hollow nanospheres were obtained.  The electrochem. properties of the amorphous TiO2 nanospheres and the TiO2 hollow nanospheres with different phases were characterized as anodes for the Na-ion batteries.  It was found that all the samples demonstrated excellent cyclability, which was sustainable for hundreds of cycles with little capacity fading, although the anatase TiO2 presented a capability that was better than that of the mixed anatase/rutile TiO2 or the amorphous TiO2@C.  Through crystallog. anal., it was revealed that the anatase TiO2 crystal structure supplies two-dimensional diffusion paths for Na-ion intercalation and more accommodation sites.  D. functional theory calcns. indicated lower energy barriers for the insertion of Na+ into anatase TiO2.  Therefore, anatase TiO2 hollow nanospheres show excellent high-rate performance.  Through ex situ field emission SEM, it was revealed that the TiO2 hollow nanosphere architecture can be maintained for hundreds of cycles, which is the main reason for its superior cyclability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1039/c3cc45254a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Memarzadeh Lotfabad, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Farbod, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kohandehghan, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mitlin, D.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanocrystalline Anatase TiO2: A New Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-organized amorphous TiO2 nanotube arrays (NTAs) were successfully fabricated on both Ti foil and porous Ti foam through electrochem. anodization techniques. The TiO2 NTAs on Ti foam were used as anodes in lithium ion batteries; they exhibited high capacities of 103 μAh cm-2 at 10 μA cm-2 and 83 μAh cm-2 at 500 μA cm-2, which are two to three times higher than those achieved on the std. Despite several studies of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries involving layered oxide materials, there are few low-voltage metal oxide anodes capable of operating sodium ion reversibly at room temp.  We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of in situ polymer-functionalized anatase TiO2 particles using an anchoring block copolymer with hydroxamate as coordinating species is reported, which yields nanoparticles (≈11 nm) in multigram scale. Downsizing the particles leads to higher reversible uptake/release of sodium cations per formula unit TiO2 (e.g., 0.72 eq.  Na(+) (11 nm) vs only 0.56 eq. The combination of small particle size and homogeneous carbon coating allows for the excellent electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 at high (134 mAh g(-1) at 10 C (3.35 A g(-1))) and low (≈227 mAh g(-1) at 0.1 C) current rates, high cycling stability (full capacity retention between 2nd and 300th cycle at 1 C) and improved coulombic efficiency (≈99.8%).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b00098"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fukunishi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Morgan, B.                         rightTab-cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase TiO2 nanoparticles for high power sodium-ion anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Liming; Buchholz, Daniel; Bresser, Dominic; Gomes Chagas, Luciana; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be a promising low-cost alternative to common lithium-ion batteries.  Herein, we present a detailed electrochem. characterization of anatase TiO2 nanoparticles as anode material for Na-ion batteries, highlighting the substantial influence of the electrolyte compn. (salt and solvent) on the obtainable specific capacity, cycling stability, and particularly the coulombic efficiency.  TiO2-based electrodes cycled using a 1M soln. of NaClO4 in a mixt. of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate present excellent high rate capability, offering about 100 and 86 mAh g-1 at 5.5 and 11 C, resp., which is - to the best of our knowledge - the best high rate capability for all titanium-based sodium-ion anode materials reported so far.  Setting the C rate to 5.5C (i.e., 1.85 A g-1), such electrodes can be cycled for more than 1000 cycles without significant capacity decay, confirming their outstanding durability at such high specific current.  These results, in combination with its environmental friendliness and cost efficiency, render anatase TiO2 nanoparticles a promising anode material for high power sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1021/nl402747x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, K.-T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ali, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chung, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V. By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the merits of good chem. stability, elemental abundance and nontoxicity, TiO2 (TiO2) has drawn increasing attraction for use as anode material in Na-ion batteries.  Nanostructured TiO2 was able to achieve high energy d.  However, nanosized TiO2 is typically electrochem. instable, which leads to poor cycling performance.  To improve the cycling stability, C from thermolysis of poly(vinyl pyrrolidone) was coated onto TiO2 nanoparticles.  Electronic cond. and electrochem. stability were enhanced by coating C onto TiO2 nanoparticles.  The resultant C-coated TiO2 nanoparticles exhibited high reversible capacity (242.3 mAh g-1), high coulombic efficiency (97.8%), and good capacity retention (87.0%) at 30 mA g-1 over 100 cycles. The rate performance of C-coated TiO2 nanoparticles was also displayed ≤127.6 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 800 mA g-1. Therefore, C-coated TiO2 nanoparticles are promising anode candidate for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2015.08.051"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Du, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">TiO2/carbon Hollow Spheres as Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A TiO2@reduced graphene oxide (TiO2@rGO) composite is synthesized successfully by a simple spray-drying method.  The structural and morphol. characterizations reveal that the TiO2@rGO composite has a sphere-like morphol. with diams. of 5-15 μm, and each microsphere is actually a random aggregation of primary TiO2 nanoparticles, which are tightly covered by soft graphene sheets.  The TiO2@rGO electrode exhibits enhanced electrochem. performance of high charge capacity (170 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C), long cycling life (87% of capacity retention after 300 cycles at 0.2 C), and high rate capability (55 mA h g-1 at 10 C).  Compared with bare TiO2 microspheres, the improved electrochem. performance of the TiO2@rGO composite benefits from the introduction of graphene as a highly conductive, flexible, and large-surface-area scaffold to provide good electronic contact between active materials, suppress the aggregation of intermediate products, and alleviate the vol. change during sodiation and desodiation.  The encouraging exptl. results suggest that the TiO2@rGO composite has great potential as a high-capacity and high-stability anode for the sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA04402F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiong, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphene-Supported TiO 2 Nanospheres as a High-Capacity and Long-Cycle Life Anode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Anatase TiO2 has low-cost and potentially high capacity as a Na-storage anode, but its low capacity utilization and poor rate capability remain a challenge for practical battery applications.  To address this issue, we synthesized graphene-supported TiO2 nanospheres through a facile hydrothermal process, aiming at enhancing the electrochem. capacity and kinetics of this material.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The main disadvantage of using transition metal oxides for Na+-ion batteries is the sluggish kinetics of insertion of Na+ ions into the structure.  Here, we introduce nanosized anatase TiO2 that is partially doped with fluorine (TiO2-δFδ) to form electro-conducting trivalent Ti3+ as an ultrafast Na+ insertion material for use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  In addn., the F-doped TiO2-δFδ is modified by electro-conducting carbon nanotubes (CNTs) to further enhance the elec. cond.  The composite F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is produced in a one-pot hydrothermal reaction.  X-ray diffraction and microscopic studies revealed that nanocryst. anatase-type TiO2-δFδ particles, in which fluorine is present with TiO2 particles, are loaded on the CNTs. As expected, a high-rate performance was achieved even at the 100 C discharging rate (25 A g-1) where the composite material demonstrated a capacity of 118 mA h g-1 under the 0.1 C-rate charge condition.  The present work also highlights a significant improvement in the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions when the material was charged and discharged under the same rate of 35 C (8.75 A g-1), delivering approx. 90 mA h (g-oxide)-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA08472A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cai, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nitrogen-Doped TiO 2 Nanospheres for Advanced Sodium-Ion Battery and Sodium-Ion Capacitor Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the aim of enhancing the electrochem. kinetics and capacity of the TiO2 electrode for Na ion batteries (NIBs), we have designed a hybrid material of carbon-coated TiO2 mesocrystals anchored on reduced graphene oxide (TiO2@C-rGO).  Such hybrid nanostructures are fabricated through a facile one-step route including in situ growth of oriented self-assembly of TiO2 mesocrystals on GO.  TiO2@C-rGO possesses a very large surface area (279 m2 g-1), mesoporous nature, and single-crystal-like structure.  It is also found that the capacity of TiO2 electrode for NIBs could be improved by carbon coating at a low current rate, but pure TiO2 shows better rate performance than that of TiO2@C.  Remarkably, the enhanced electrochem. kinetics and large capacity can be simultaneously achieved by designing hybrid material.  The hybrid nanostructures exhibit a highly reversible capacity of 300 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, superior rate capability, and long-term cycling stability (a stable capacity of 159 mAh g-1 can be maintained after 1000 cycles at 1 A g-1).  The superior Na ion storage of TiO2@C-rGO is largely ascribed to the robust architecture of well-dispersed carbon-coated mesoporous TiO2 mesocrystals anchored on conductive graphene network, leading to enhanced electrochem. kinetics and offering enough active sites for the Na ion to locate.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.06.150"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tan, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-Pot Synthesis of Bicrystalline Titanium Dioxide Spheres with a Core–shell Structure as Anode Materials for Lithium and Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the aim of advancing anatase TiO2 anodes for Na ion batteries, cryst. TiO2 nanocubes were employed and they delivered a gradually increasing capacity during the initial cycles, termed as an activation process.  The no. of necessary discharge-charge loops for total activation is dependent on the galvanostatic c.d. (∼20 cycles at 0.2 C, or 90 cycles at 1 C).  A percentage of Ti3+ was detected after the activation, indicating an amt. of irreversibly trapped Na ions in the lattice.  After the activation process, an excellent rate capability and outstanding cycling stability were presented.  The reversible capacity reached 174, 132, and 108 mA h g-1 at rates of 1 C, 5 C, and 10 C, resp.  The capacity was sustained with a loss of &lt;10% after 1000 discharge-charge cycles at a rate of 2 C or 10 C.  The superior battery performance achieved by the nanocubes is related to the encircled {100} facets that are more favorable for Na ion attachment compared to the {001} and {101} facets, as supported by 1st-principles calcns.  From this work the authors can see the feasibility of optimizing electrode materials via rational surface structure construction based on theor. calcns.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201504412"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Maier, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Self-Supported Nanotube Arrays of Sulfur-Doped TiO 2 Enabling Ultrastable and Robust Sodium Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In an attempt to realize Na-ion batteries with enhanced safety, the authors report herein the use of ionic liq. (IL)-based electrolytes for cycling nanoparticulate anatase TiO2 as Na-ion anode material.  The use of the IL-based electrolyte results in the highly stable cycling performance of anatase TiO2-based electrodes, providing an initial specific capacity of 159 mAh g-1 when applying a specific current of 33.5 mA g-1, and still 155 mAh g-1 after 80 full (dis-)charge cycles.  Also, a very promising rate performance was obtained with specific capacities of 108 and 78 mAh g-1 for specific currents of 335 and 670 mA g-1, resp.  Indeed, the excellent electrochem. performance, and high coulombic efficiency, as well as the electrochem. impedance spectroscopy results indicate the highly beneficial impact of the IL-based electrolyte on the formation of a stabilized solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00491"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mogensen, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Brandell, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Younesi, R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Solubility of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase (SEI) in Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Energy Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">1</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1173</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1178</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00491</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solubility of the Solid Electrolyte Interphase (SEI) in Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mogensen, Ronnie; Brandell, Daniel; Younesi, Reza</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1173-1178</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AELCCP</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2380-8195</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is often stated that formation of a functional solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) in sodium ion batteries is hampered by the higher soly. of SEI components such as sodium salts in comparison to the lithium analogs.  In order to investigate these phenomena, SEI formation and functionality, as well as cell self-discharge, are studied for the sodium ion system with comparative expts. on the equiv. lithium ion system.  By conducting a set of expts. on carbonaceous anodes, the impact of SEI dissoln. is tested.  The results show that the SEI layer in sodium ion cells is inferior to that in lithium ion counterparts with regards to self-discharge; sodium cells show a loss in capacity at a dramatic rate as compared to the lithium counterparts when they are stored at sodiated and lithiated states, resp., for a long time with no external applied current or potential.  Also, synchrotron-based hard XPS measurements indicate that the major factor leading to increased self-discharge is dissoln. of significant parts of the sodium-based SEI.  Furthermore, the effect of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) electrolyte additive on self-discharge is tested as part of the work.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1039/c4cp00826j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tokiwa, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Negative Electrodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comparative study of the electrode/electrolyte interface was carried out for lithium and sodium metal anodes in electrolytes consisting in 1 M LiPF6 in EC0.5:DMC0.5 (LP30) and 1 M NaPF6 in both EC0.5:DMC0.5 and EC0.45PC0.45DMC0.1.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Korean Society of Industrial and Engineering Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of the electrolyte systems used for lithium-ion batteries.  The problems confronting current carbonate-based electrolytes are discussed in terms of following aspects including capacity, temp. limit, safety and hazard, and ion transport.  Various efforts to apply room temp. ionic liqs. for electrolytes of lithium-ion batteries are introduced due to their excellent electrochem. properties such as non-flammability, non-volatility, chem. inertness, wide electrochem. windows, high cond., and wide thermal operating ranges.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1002/9781118615515.ch14"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guerfi, A.; Vijh, A.; Zaghib, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Safe Lithium Rechargeable Batteries Based on Ionic Liquids</span>.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society of Japan</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of ionic liqs. (ILs) and their applications as electrolytes for elec. double layer capacitors, fuel cells, Li batteries, and solar cells is presented.  The properties of ILs, such as non-volatility, non-flammability, wide liq. temp. range, and wide electrochem. windows, can be improved, including improvement of durability and safety, extending the operational temp. ranges and enabling improvements in power and energy densities of the devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2009.06.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lewandowski, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Świderska-Mocek, A.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ionic Liquids as Electrolytes for Li-Ion batteries—An Overview of Electrochemical Studies</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The paper reviews properties of room temp. ionic liqs. (RTILs) as electrolytes for lithium and lithium-ion batteries.  It has been shown that the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the anode surface is crit. to the correct operation of secondary lithium-ion batteries, including those working with ionic liqs. as electrolytes.  The SEI layer may be formed by electrochem. transformation of (i) a mol. additive, (ii) RTIL cations or (iii) RTIL anions.  Such properties of RTIL electrolytes as viscosity, cond., vapor pressure and lithium-ion transport nos. are also discussed from the point of view of their influence on battery performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.03.089"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nohira, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kuroda, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hagiwara, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fukunaga, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sakai, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nitta, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Inazawa, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NaFSA–C1C3pyrFSA Ionic Liquids for Sodium Secondary Battery Operating over a Wide Temperature Range</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                 10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.03.089</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA ionic liquids for sodium secondary battery operating over a wide temperature range</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Changsheng; Nohira, Toshiyuki; Kuroda, Keisuke; Hagiwara, Rika; Fukunaga, Atsushi; Sakai, Shoichiro; Nitta, Koji; Inazawa, Shinji</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">238</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">296-300</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The physicochem. properties of NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA ionic liqs. were investigated to explore their potential as new electrolytes for sodium secondary batteries operating over a wide temp. range.  The viscosity and ionic cond. of NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA with a molar ratio of 2:8 were 16.7 cP and 15.6 mS cm-1, resp., at 353 K.  The electrochem. window of this ionic liq. was 5.2 V at 353 K with sodium metal deposition detg. the cathode limit.  A Na/NaFSA-C1C3pyrFSA/NaCrO2 cell exhibited stable charge-discharge behavior at 298 and 353 K.  The discharge capacities of 92 and 106 mAh (g-NaCrO2)-1 were obsd. at 298 and 353 K, resp., at 20 mA g-1.  The Coulombic efficiency was higher than 99% during the charge-discharge tests except for the initial few cycles.  The cell also showed high discharge rates, reaching 500 mA g-1 at 353 K.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref57" class="rightTabRefNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit57" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.09.106"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Amarilla, J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this paper we report on the investigation of ionic liq.-based electrolytes with enhanced characteristics.  In particular, we have studied ternary mixts. based on the lithium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) salt and two ionic liqs. sharing the same cation (N-methyl-N-Pr pyrrolidinium, PYR13), but different anions, bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (TFSI) and bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (FSI).  The LiTFSI-PYR13TFSI-PYR13FSI mixts., found to be ionically dissocd., exhibit better ion transport properties (about 10-3 S cm-1 at -20°C) with respect to similar ionic liq. electrolytes till reported in literature.  An electrochem. stability window of 5 V is obsd. in carbon working electrodes.  Preliminary battery tests confirm the good performance of these ternary electrolytes with high-voltage NMC cathodes and graphite anodes.  Ionic liq. electrolyte mixts., PYR13TFSI, PYR13FSI.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201103168"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tartaj, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Amarilla, J. These 25 nm nanostructures show good electrochem. performance and good capabilities for enzyme immobilization and photocatalytic degrdn.  Multifunctionality, together with small size (25 nm), controlled microstructure (uniform particles and crystallinity), and good textural properties (BET surfaces area above 150 m2/g with pore sizes above 4 nm), make these materials potential candidates for energy storage devices (esp. if routes are applied to assemble them into porous macrostructures), for photocatalysis, and for enzyme immobilization.  When properly functionalized they could be used for photoelectrochem. and healthcare applications such as photodynamic therapy.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref62" class="rightTabRefNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit62" data-doi="10.1039/C0CC01540G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tartaj, P.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sub-100 Nm TiO 2 Mesocrystalline Assemblies with Mesopores: Preparation, Characterization, Enzyme Immobilization and Photocatalytic Properties</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         <script nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);

        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="9318acec7a5888d3-MAN">
var currentJournal = "ACS Omega";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
